Positioned approximately 100 meters above the CMS collision point, MATHUSLA features a substantial decay volume roughly 40 meters by 40 meters in footprint and 11 meters tall, optimized to observe LLP decays occurring at macroscopic distances outside the main LHC detectors.\n\nThe detector is instrumented with layers of plastic scintillator tracker modules on its ceiling and rear wall, read out by Silicon Photomultipliers (SiPMs) for precise timing and spatial resolution. It operates without a magnetic field, functioning primarily as a pure decay vertex detector, and employs extensive cosmic ray veto systems to suppress backgrounds, effectively differentiating upward-moving decay products of LLPs from downward cosmic-ray showers. The modular design of MATHUSLA allows adaptability to civil engineering constraints, and it is synchronized with the LHC clock, enabling correlated triggering of the CMS detector for combined event analysis.\n\nMATHUSLA’s primary scientific goal is the discovery and study of LLPs with displaced decays that escape detection in ATLAS and CMS, extending the lifetime reach of LLP searches by one to two orders of magnitude and significantly enhancing sensitivity to LLP production rates beyond the capabilities of existing detectors. It excels particularly in detecting LLPs produced in exotic decays of the Higgs boson, top quarks, and heavy exotic resonances at 14 TeV proton-proton collisions, including scenarios involving Higgs-portal scalars, neutral naturalness theories predicting long-lived hidden hadrons, and certain supersymmetric particles with moderate lifetimes. The detector is optimized for neutral LLPs with masses in the range from tens to hundreds of GeV and sensitivity to hadronic final states, covering a pseudorapidity range approximately between 0 and 2.\n\nWhile MATHUSLA is primarily designed for collider physics, it has additional capabilities for detecting some cosmic-ray events or exotic cosmic phenomena. Although its acceptance for certain light long-lived particles, such as light Heavy Neutral Leptons (HNLs) or dark photons, is more limited compared to specialized forward detectors or experiments like SHiP, it may still detect heavier HNLs produced in exotic W or Z boson decays with displaced vertices outside CMS.\n\nIn summary,', 'source_id': 'chunk-29fdbe0c25e6d2f9b6863aeea6f5559e<SEP>chunk-e320ca0da34959051f20c9bf84edacd6<SEP>chunk-b5a2331e56271f59a730032b1586242a<SEP>chunk-5292ea0990d4e0f1654fe576d320a493<SEP>chunk-b99d3d91dfde8a6a3a3b72928171fb89<SEP>chunk-aebd96c6eaa22d67b1274449801c10db<SEP>chunk-cacb10d74a68ab3512c23db17c518682<SEP>chunk-a954894c433285daad9551ac9df6f00d<SEP>chunk-30543c07ca44d0768b41c9ddfdc4cca6<SEP>chunk-f699be222a0055d03a932562539cc24a<SEP>chunk-2444cab01ca6c98a525bcdaab9a14ff7<SEP>chunk-91fc821bb44e5e4024a7406f4b60b537<SEP>chunk-eaf2b3840fb77242f41031b75ff406df<SEP>chunk-64056261913e790c5951c77223367959', 'file_path': 'unknown_source', 'created_at': 1747475375} |

It is designed to exploit an intensity-frontier approach by using a 400 GeV proton beam dumped on a very dense, likely hybrid molybdenum/tungsten target to produce an intense flux of heavy mesons (such as charm and bottom hadrons) and light mesons (pions, kaons). This setup enables the production and study of feebly interacting, long-lived particles (LLPs) in the sub-10 GeV mass range, particularly between approximately 0.1 and 10 GeV/c².\n\nThe primary scientific goal of SHiP is to search for rare and exotic particles beyond the Standard Model (BSM), including Heavy Neutral Leptons (HNLs), dark photons (vector portal states), light neutralinos, sgoldstinos, millicharged particles, and light dark matter particles. SHiP is especially well suited to probe “mini-SUSY” scenarios and other models with tiny couplings down to extremely small mixing angles (e.g., U² ~ 10⁻¹⁰), covering nearly the entire parameter space relevant to explaining neutrino masses via the see-saw mechanism and potentially addressing leptogenic baryogenesis. Its sensitivity surpasses that of other experiments such as NA62 and flavor-factory experiments like Belle II, and it offers complementary physics reach compared to LHC-based efforts including detectors like MATHUSLA.\n\nThe experimental facility consists of multiple integrated detection systems within a dedicated underground complex. Key components include the dense proton target and hadron absorber to minimize secondary particle backgrounds (especially neutrinos), a custom muon shield designed to drastically reduce the muon flux, and a large (approximately 50 m) decay volume where LLP decays can be observed with minimal background. The detector setup features sophisticated magnetic spectrometry and calorimetry systems combined with scintillating taggers, layered veto detectors, and precise timing systems that enable near background-free operation by discriminating cosmic rays and other spurious signals.\n\nSHiP’s detection capabilities include excellent reconstruction of displaced vertices from multi-track LLP decays, including hadronic, leptonic, and electromagnetic channels, with precise measurement of decay kinematics and particle masses. Moreover, the experiment incorporates a dedicated neutrino detector downstream to study tau neutrino and anti', 'source_id': 'chunk-29fdbe0c25e6d2f9b6863aeea6f5559e<SEP>chunk-e320ca0da34959051f20c9bf84edacd6<SEP>chunk-b5a2331e56271f59a730032b1586242a<SEP>chunk-b3d7789b409691870a7a98fc35b21500<SEP>chunk-5292ea0990d4e0f1654fe576d320a493<SEP>chunk-b99d3d91dfde8a6a3a3b72928171fb89<SEP>chunk-aebd96c6eaa22d67b1274449801c10db<SEP>chunk-cacb10d74a68ab3512c23db17c518682<SEP>chunk-a954894c433285daad9551ac9df6f00d<SEP>chunk-30543c07ca44d0768b41c9ddfdc4cca6<SEP>chunk-f699be222a0055d03a932562539cc24a<SEP>chunk-2444cab01ca6c98a525bcdaab9a14ff7<SEP>chunk-91fc821bb44e5e4024a7406f4b60b537<SEP>chunk-eaf2b3840fb77242f41031b75ff406df<SEP>chunk-cbad92c147298b6fb1914634317f43d7<SEP>chunk-64056261913e790c5951c77223367959', 'file_path': 'unknown_source', 'created_at': 1747475387} |
